<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nominations">Nominations</span></h3>
<p>Nomination forms are sent by the Nobel Committee to about 3,000 individuals, usually in September the year before the prizes are awarded. These individuals are generally prominent academics working in a relevant area. Regarding the Peace Prize, inquiries are also sent to governments, former Peace Prize laureates, and current or former members of the Norwegian Nobel Committee. The deadline for the return of the nomination forms is 31 January of the year of the award.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman16-17_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman16-17-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Levinovitz26_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levinovitz26-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The Nobel Committee nominates about 300 potential laureates from these forms and additional names.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams15_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams15-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The nominees are not publicly named, nor are they told that they are being considered for the prize. All nomination records for a prize are sealed for 50 years from the awarding of the prize.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman315_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman315-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Selection">Selection</span></h3>
<p>The Nobel Committee then prepares a report reflecting the advice of experts in the relevant fields. This, along with the list of preliminary candidates, is submitted to the prize-awarding institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman52_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman52-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The institutions meet to choose the laureate or laureates in each field by a majority vote. Their decision, which cannot be appealed, is announced immediately after the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-Levinovitz25-28_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levinovitz25-28-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> A maximum of three laureates and two different works may be selected per award. Except for the Peace Prize, which can be awarded to institutions, the awards can only be given to individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams8_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams8-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Posthumous_nominations">Posthumous nominations</span></h3>
<p>Although posthumous nominations are not presently permitted, individuals who died in the months between their nomination and the decision of the prize committee were originally eligible to receive the prize. This has occurred twice: the 1931 Literature Prize awarded to <a href="/wiki/Erik_Axel_Karlfeldt" title="Erik Axel Karlfeldt">Erik Axel Karlfeldt</a>, and the 1961 Peace Prize awarded to <a href="/wiki/Secretary-General_of_the_United_Nations" title="Secretary-General of the United Nations">UN Secretary General</a> <a href="/wiki/Dag_Hammarskj%C3%B6ld" title="Dag Hammarskjöld">Dag Hammarskjöld</a>. Since 1974, laureates must be thought alive at the time of the October announcement. There has been one laureate, <a href="/wiki/William_Vickrey" title="William Vickrey">William Vickrey</a>, who in 1996 died after the prize (in <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Memorial_Prize_in_Economic_Sciences" title="Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences">Economics</a>) was announced but before it could be presented.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams9_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams9-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> On 3 October 2011, the laureates for the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a> were announced; however, the committee was not aware that one of the laureates, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_M._Steinman" title="Ralph M. Steinman">Ralph M. Steinman</a>, had died three days earlier. The committee was debating about Steinman's prize, since the rule is that the prize is not awarded posthumously.<sup id="cite_ref-cbc1003_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbc1003-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The committee later decided that as the decision to award Steinman the prize "was made in good faith", it would remain unchanged.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition_time_lag">Recognition time lag</span></h3>
<p>Nobel's will provided for prizes to be awarded in recognition of discoveries made "during the preceding year". Early on, the awards usually recognised recent discoveries.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> However, some of those early discoveries were later discredited. For example, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Fibiger" title="Johannes Fibiger">Johannes Fibiger</a> was awarded the 1926 Prize in <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Physiology or Medicine</a> for his purported discovery of a parasite that caused cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-Levinovitz125_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levinovitz125-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> To avoid repeating this embarrassment, the awards increasingly recognised scientific discoveries that had withstood the test of time.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams25_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams25-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-embo_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embo-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-scienceline1_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scienceline1-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> According to Ralf Pettersson, former chairman of the Nobel Prize Committee for Physiology or Medicine, "the criterion 'the previous year' is interpreted by the Nobel Assembly as the year when the full impact of the discovery has become evident."<sup id="cite_ref-embo_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embo-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nobelinstituttet_20080913-01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A room with pictures on the walls. In the middle of the room there is a wooden table with chairs around it." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Nobelinstituttet_20080913-01.jpg/220px-Nobelinstituttet_20080913-01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Nobelinstituttet_20080913-01.jpg/330px-Nobelinstituttet_20080913-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Nobelinstituttet_20080913-01.jpg/440px-Nobelinstituttet_20080913-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4368" data-file-height="2912" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nobelinstituttet_20080913-01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The committee room of the Norwegian Nobel Committee</div></div></div>
<p>The interval between the award and the accomplishment it recognises varies from discipline to discipline. The Literature Prize is typically awarded to recognise a cumulative lifetime body of work rather than a single achievement.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The Peace Prize can also be awarded for a lifetime body of work. For example, 2008 laureate <a href="/wiki/Martti_Ahtisaari" title="Martti Ahtisaari">Martti Ahtisaari</a> was awarded for his work to resolve international conflicts.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> However, they can also be awarded for specific recent events.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> For instance, <a href="/wiki/Kofi_Annan" title="Kofi Annan">Kofi Annan</a> was awarded the 2001 Peace Prize just four years after becoming the Secretary-General of the United Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams330_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams330-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Similarly <a href="/wiki/Yasser_Arafat" title="Yasser Arafat">Yasser Arafat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yitzhak_Rabin" title="Yitzhak Rabin">Yitzhak Rabin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shimon_Peres" title="Shimon Peres">Shimon Peres</a> received the 1994 award, about a year after they successfully concluded the <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Accords" title="Oslo Accords">Oslo Accords</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams27_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams27-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Awards for physics, chemistry, and medicine are typically awarded once the achievement has been widely accepted. Sometimes, this takes decades – for example, <a href="/wiki/Subrahmanyan_Chandrasekhar" title="Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar">Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar</a> shared the 1983 Physics Prize for his 1930s work on stellar structure and evolution.<sup id="cite_ref-currentscience_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-currentscience-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Not all scientists live long enough for their work to be recognised. Some discoveries can never be considered for a prize if their impact is realised after the discoverers have died.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Award_ceremonies">Award ceremonies</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:293px;max-width:293px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:117px;max-width:117px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Horbjorn_Jagland_presents_President_Barack_Obama_with_the_Nobel_Prize_medal_and_diploma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Two men standing on a stage. The man to the left is clapping his hands and looking towards the other man. The second man is smiling and showing two items to an audience not seen on the image. The items are a diploma which includes a painting and a box containing a gold medal. Behind them is a blue pillar clad in flowers." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Horbjorn_Jagland_presents_President_Barack_Obama_with_the_Nobel_Prize_medal_and_diploma.jpg/115px-Horbjorn_Jagland_presents_President_Barack_Obama_with_the_Nobel_Prize_medal_and_diploma.jpg" decoding="async" width="115" height="172" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Horbjorn_Jagland_presents_President_Barack_Obama_with_the_Nobel_Prize_medal_and_diploma.jpg/173px-Horbjorn_Jagland_presents_President_Barack_Obama_with_the_Nobel_Prize_medal_and_diploma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Horbjorn_Jagland_presents_President_Barack_Obama_with_the_Nobel_Prize_medal_and_diploma.jpg/230px-Horbjorn_Jagland_presents_President_Barack_Obama_with_the_Nobel_Prize_medal_and_diploma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2731" data-file-height="4096" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:172px;max-width:172px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Giovanni_Jona-Lasinio-Nobel_Lecture-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A man in his fifties standing behind a desk with computers on it. On the desk is a sign reading &quot;Kungl. Vetensk. Akad. Sigil&quot;." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Giovanni_Jona-Lasinio-Nobel_Lecture-2.jpg/170px-Giovanni_Jona-Lasinio-Nobel_Lecture-2.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="146" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Giovanni_Jona-Lasinio-Nobel_Lecture-2.jpg/255px-Giovanni_Jona-Lasinio-Nobel_Lecture-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Giovanni_Jona-Lasinio-Nobel_Lecture-2.jpg/340px-Giovanni_Jona-Lasinio-Nobel_Lecture-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1602" data-file-height="1375" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:;background-color:">Left: Barack Obama after receiving the Nobel Peace Prize in Oslo City Hall from the hands of Norwegian Nobel Committee Chairman <a href="/wiki/Thorbj%C3%B8rn_Jagland" title="Thorbjørn Jagland">Thorbjørn Jagland</a> in 2009; Right: <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Jona-Lasinio" title="Giovanni Jona-Lasinio">Giovanni Jona-Lasinio</a> presenting <a href="/wiki/Yoichiro_Nambu" title="Yoichiro Nambu">Yoichiro Nambu</a>'s Nobel Lecture at <a href="/wiki/Aula_Magna_(Stockholm_University)" title="Aula Magna (Stockholm University)">Aula Magna</a>, Stockholm in 2008</div></div></div></div>
<p>Except for the Peace Prize, the Nobel Prizes are presented in Stockholm, Sweden, at the annual Prize Award Ceremony on 10 December, the anniversary of Nobel's death. The recipients' lectures are normally held in the days prior to the award ceremony. The Peace Prize and its recipients' lectures are presented at the annual Prize Award Ceremony in Oslo, Norway, usually on 10 December. The award ceremonies and the associated banquets are typically major international events.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thelocal2_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thelocal2-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The Prizes awarded in Sweden's ceremonies' are held at the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_Concert_Hall" title="Stockholm Concert Hall">Stockholm Concert Hall</a>, with the Nobel banquet following immediately at <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_City_Hall" title="Stockholm City Hall">Stockholm City Hall</a>. The Nobel Peace Prize ceremony has been held at the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Nobel_Institute" title="Norwegian Nobel Institute">Norwegian Nobel Institute</a> (1905–1946), at the <a href="/wiki/Auditorium" title="Auditorium">auditorium</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oslo" title="University of Oslo">University of Oslo</a> (1947–1989), and at <a href="/wiki/Oslo_City_Hall" title="Oslo City Hall">Oslo City Hall</a> (1990–present).<sup id="cite_ref-Levinovitz21-23_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levinovitz21-23-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The highlight of the Nobel Prize Award Ceremony in Stockholm occurs when each Nobel laureate steps forward to receive the prize from the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Sweden" title="Monarchy of Sweden">King of Sweden</a>. In Oslo, the Chairman of the Norwegian Nobel Committee presents the Nobel Peace Prize in the presence of the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Norway" title="Monarchy of Norway">King of Norway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thelocal2_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thelocal2-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sweden2_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sweden2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> At first, King <a href="/wiki/Oscar_II_of_Sweden" title="Oscar II of Sweden">Oscar II</a> did not approve of awarding grand prizes to foreigners. It is said<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (December 2018)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> that he changed his mind once his attention had been drawn to the publicity value of the prizes for Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nobel_Banquet">Nobel Banquet</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Banquet" title="Nobel Banquet">Nobel Banquet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nobel-banquet-table.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A set table with a white table cloth. There are many plates and glasses plus a menu visible on the table." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Nobel-banquet-table.jpg/220px-Nobel-banquet-table.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Nobel-banquet-table.jpg/330px-Nobel-banquet-table.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Nobel-banquet-table.jpg/440px-Nobel-banquet-table.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nobel-banquet-table.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Table at the 2005 Nobel Banquet in Stockholm</div></div></div>
<p>After the award ceremony in Sweden, a banquet is held in the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Hall" title="Blue Hall">Blue Hall</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_City_Hall" title="Stockholm City Hall">Stockholm City Hall</a>, which is attended by the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Royal_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="Swedish Royal Family">Swedish Royal Family</a> and around 1,300 guests. The <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> banquet is held in Norway at the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Hotel_(Oslo)" title="Grand Hotel (Oslo)">Oslo Grand Hotel</a> after the award ceremony. Apart from the laureate, guests include the President of the <a href="/wiki/Storting" title="Storting">Storting</a>, on occasion the Swedish prime minister, and, since 2006, the King and Queen of Norway. In total, about 250 guests attend.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nobel_lecture">Nobel lecture</span></h3>
<p>According to the statutes of the Nobel Foundation, each laureate is required to give a public lecture on a subject related to the topic of their prize.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The Nobel lecture as a rhetorical genre took decades to reach its current format.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> These lectures normally occur during Nobel Week (the week leading up to the award ceremony and banquet, which begins with the laureates arriving in Stockholm and normally ends with the Nobel banquet), but this is not mandatory. The laureate is only obliged to give the lecture within six months of receiving the prize, but some have happened even later. For example, US President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> received the Peace Prize in 1906 but gave his lecture in 1910, after his term in office.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams18-19_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams18-19-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The lectures are organized by the same association which selected the laureates.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prizes">Prizes</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medals">Medals</span></h3>
<p>The Nobel Foundation announced on 30 May 2012 that it had awarded the contract for the production of the five (Swedish) Nobel Prize medals to Svenska Medalj AB. Between 1902 and 2010, the Nobel Prize medals were minted by <a href="/wiki/Myntverket" title="Myntverket">Myntverket</a> (the Swedish Mint), Sweden's oldest company, which ceased operations in 2011 after 107 years. In 2011, the Mint of Norway, located in Kongsberg, made the medals. The Nobel Prize medals are registered trademarks of the Nobel Foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Each medal features an image of Alfred Nobel in left profile on the <a href="/wiki/Obverse_and_reverse" title="Obverse and reverse">obverse</a>. The medals for physics, chemistry, physiology or medicine, and literature have identical obverses, showing the image of Alfred Nobel and the years of his birth and death. Nobel's portrait also appears on the obverse of the Peace Prize medal and the medal for the Economics Prize, but with a slightly different design. For instance, the laureate's name is engraved on the rim of the Economics medal.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman2_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The image on the reverse of a medal varies according to the institution awarding the prize. The reverse sides of the medals for chemistry and physics share the same design.<sup id="cite_ref-Paulingmedal_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paulingmedal-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nobel_Prize_Diploma_Fritz_Haber_1918.JPG" class="image"><img alt="A heavily decorated paper with the name &quot;Fritz Haber&quot; on it." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Nobel_Prize_Diploma_Fritz_Haber_1918.JPG/220px-Nobel_Prize_Diploma_Fritz_Haber_1918.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Nobel_Prize_Diploma_Fritz_Haber_1918.JPG/330px-Nobel_Prize_Diploma_Fritz_Haber_1918.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Nobel_Prize_Diploma_Fritz_Haber_1918.JPG/440px-Nobel_Prize_Diploma_Fritz_Haber_1918.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1938" data-file-height="1296" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nobel_Prize_Diploma_Fritz_Haber_1918.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Laureates receive a heavily decorated diploma together with a gold medal and the prize money. Here <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Haber" title="Fritz Haber">Fritz Haber</a>'s diploma is shown, which he received for the development of a method to synthesise ammonia.</div></div></div>
<p>All medals made before 1980 were struck in 23 <a href="/wiki/Carat_(purity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Carat (purity)">carat</a> gold. Since then, they have been struck in 18 carat <a href="/wiki/Coloured_gold#Green_gold" class="mw-redirect" title="Coloured gold">green gold</a> plated with 24 carat gold. The weight of each medal varies with the value of gold, but averages about 175 grams (0.386&#160;lb) for each medal. The diameter is 66 millimetres (2.6&#160;in) and the thickness varies between 5.2 millimetres (0.20&#160;in) and 2.4 millimetres (0.094&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Because of the high value of their gold content and tendency to be on public display, Nobel medals are subject to medal theft.<sup id="cite_ref-UniversityofCalifornia_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UniversityofCalifornia-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> During World War II, the medals of German scientists <a href="/wiki/Max_von_Laue" title="Max von Laue">Max von Laue</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Franck" title="James Franck">James Franck</a> were sent to Copenhagen for safekeeping. When Germany invaded Denmark, Hungarian chemist (and Nobel laureate himself) <a href="/wiki/George_de_Hevesy" title="George de Hevesy">George de Hevesy</a> dissolved them in <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">aqua regia</a> (nitro-hydrochloric acid), to prevent confiscation by <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> and to prevent legal problems for the holders. After the war, the gold was recovered from solution, and the medals re-cast.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman397_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman397-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diplomas">Diplomas</span></h3>
<p>Nobel laureates receive a diploma directly from the hands of the King of Sweden, or in the case of the peace prize, the Chairman of the Norwegian Nobel Committee. Each diploma is uniquely designed by the prize-awarding institutions for the laureates that receive them.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman2_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The diploma contains a picture and text in Swedish which states the name of the laureate and normally a citation of why they received the prize. None of the Nobel Peace Prize laureates has ever had a citation on their diplomas.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams18_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams18-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Award_money">Award money</span></h3>
<p>The laureates are given a sum of money when they receive their prizes, in the form of a document confirming the amount awarded.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman2_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The amount of prize money depends upon how much money the Nobel Foundation can award each year. The purse has increased since the 1980s, when the prize money was 880,000 SEK per prize (c. 2.6 million SEK altogether, US$350,000 today). In 2009, the monetary award was 10 million SEK (US$1.4 million).<sup id="cite_ref-Nobel_Prize_Amounts_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel_Prize_Amounts-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> In June 2012, it was lowered to 8 million SEK.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> If two laureates share the prize in a category, the award grant is divided equally between the recipients. If there are three, the awarding committee has the option of dividing the grant equally, or awarding one-half to one recipient and one-quarter to each of the others.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams8-10_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams8-10-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> It is common for recipients to donate prize money to benefit scientific, cultural, or humanitarian causes.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies_and_criticisms">Controversies and criticisms</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_controversies" title="Nobel Prize controversies">Nobel Prize controversies</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversial_recipients">Controversial recipients</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_A._Kissinger,_U.S._Secretary_of_State,_1973-1977.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Henry_A._Kissinger%2C_U.S._Secretary_of_State%2C_1973-1977.jpg/170px-Henry_A._Kissinger%2C_U.S._Secretary_of_State%2C_1973-1977.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Henry_A._Kissinger%2C_U.S._Secretary_of_State%2C_1973-1977.jpg/255px-Henry_A._Kissinger%2C_U.S._Secretary_of_State%2C_1973-1977.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Henry_A._Kissinger%2C_U.S._Secretary_of_State%2C_1973-1977.jpg/340px-Henry_A._Kissinger%2C_U.S._Secretary_of_State%2C_1973-1977.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="2459" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_A._Kissinger,_U.S._Secretary_of_State,_1973-1977.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>When it was announced that <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> was to be awarded the Peace Prize, two of the Norwegian Nobel Committee members resigned in protest.</div></div></div>
<p>Among other criticisms, the Nobel Committees have been accused of having a political agenda, and of omitting more deserving candidates. They have also been accused of <a href="/wiki/Eurocentrism" title="Eurocentrism">Eurocentrism</a>, especially for the Literature Prize.<sup id="cite_ref-Abramsxiv_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abramsxiv-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Feldman65_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman65-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sarasota_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sarasota-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Peace Prize</dt></dl>
<p>Among the most criticised Nobel Peace Prizes was the one awarded to <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> and <a href="/wiki/L%C3%AA_%C4%90%E1%BB%A9c_Th%E1%BB%8D" title="Lê Đức Thọ">Lê Đức Thọ</a>. This led to the resignation of two Norwegian Nobel Committee members.<sup id="cite_ref-topten_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-topten-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Kissinger and Thọ were awarded the prize for negotiating a ceasefire between <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">North Vietnam</a> and the United States in January 1973. However, when the award was announced, both sides were still engaging in hostilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams219_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams219-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Critics sympathetic to the North announced that Kissinger was not a peace-maker but the opposite, responsible for widening the war. Those hostile to the North and what they considered its deceptive practices during negotiations were deprived of a chance to criticise Lê Đức Thọ, as he declined the award.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman315_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman315-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abrams315_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams315-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Yasser_Arafat" title="Yasser Arafat">Yasser Arafat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shimon_Peres" title="Shimon Peres">Shimon Peres</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yitzhak_Rabin" title="Yitzhak Rabin">Yitzhak Rabin</a> received the Peace Prize in 1994 for their efforts in making peace between Israel and Palestine.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman315_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman315-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Levinovitz183_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levinovitz183-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Immediately after the award was announced, one of the five Norwegian Nobel Committee members denounced Arafat as a terrorist and resigned.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman15-16_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman15-16-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Additional misgivings about Arafat were widely expressed in various newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams302-306_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams302-306-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another controversial Peace Prize was that awarded to <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> <a href="/wiki/2009_Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="2009 Nobel Peace Prize">in 2009</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Nominations had closed only eleven days after Obama took office as <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>, but the actual evaluation occurred over the next eight months.<sup id="cite_ref-philp_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-philp-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Obama himself stated that he did not feel deserving of the award, or worthy of the company in which it would place him.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Past Peace Prize laureates were divided, some saying that Obama deserved the award, and others saying he had not secured the achievements to yet merit such an accolade. Obama's award, along with the previous Peace Prizes for <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a>, also prompted accusations of a <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">left-wing</a> bias.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Literature Prize</dt></dl>
<p>The award of the 2004 Literature Prize to <a href="/wiki/Elfriede_Jelinek" title="Elfriede Jelinek">Elfriede Jelinek</a> drew a protest from a member of the Swedish Academy, <a href="/wiki/Knut_Ahnlund" title="Knut Ahnlund">Knut Ahnlund</a>. Ahnlund resigned, alleging that the selection of Jelinek had caused "irreparable damage to all progressive forces, it has also confused the general view of literature as an art". He alleged that Jelinek's works were "a mass of text shovelled together without artistic structure".<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The 2009 Literature Prize to <a href="/wiki/Herta_M%C3%BCller" title="Herta Müller">Herta Müller</a> also generated criticism. According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>, many US literary critics and professors were ignorant of her work.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> This made those critics feel the prizes were too Eurocentric.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Science prizes</dt></dl>
<p>In 1949, the neurologist <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_Egas_Moniz" title="António Egas Moniz">António Egas Moniz</a> received the Physiology or Medicine Prize for his development of the <a href="/wiki/Lobotomy" title="Lobotomy">prefrontal leucotomy</a>. The previous year, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Jackson_Freeman_II" title="Walter Jackson Freeman II">Dr. Walter Freeman</a> had developed a <a href="/wiki/Lobotomy#Transorbital_lobotomy" title="Lobotomy">version of the procedure</a> which was faster and easier to carry out. Due in part to the publicity surrounding the original procedure, Freeman's procedure was prescribed without due consideration or regard for modern <a href="/wiki/Medical_ethics" title="Medical ethics">medical ethics</a>. Endorsed by such influential publications as <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_England_Journal_of_Medicine" title="The New England Journal of Medicine">The New England Journal of Medicine</a></i>, leucotomy or "lobotomy" became so popular that about 5,000 lobotomies were performed in the United States in the three years immediately following Moniz's receipt of the Prize.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman286-289_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman286-289-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overlooked_achievements">Overlooked achievements</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_Gandhi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Portrait_Gandhi.jpg/170px-Portrait_Gandhi.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Portrait_Gandhi.jpg/255px-Portrait_Gandhi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Portrait_Gandhi.jpg/340px-Portrait_Gandhi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2024" data-file-height="3040" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_Gandhi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Norwegian Nobel Committee declined to award a prize in 1948, the year of Gandhi's death, on the grounds that "there was no suitable living candidate."</div></div></div>
<p>The Norwegian Nobel Committee confirmed that <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> was nominated for the Peace Prize in 1937–39, 1947 and, a few days before he was assassinated in January 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-Levinovitz181-186_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levinovitz181-186-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Later, members of the Norwegian Nobel Committee expressed regret that he was not given the prize.<sup id="cite_ref-Gandhi_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gandhi-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Geir Lundestad, Secretary of Norwegian Nobel Committee in 2006, said, "The greatest omission in our 106 year history is undoubtedly that Mahatma Gandhi never received the Nobel Peace prize. Gandhi could do without the Nobel Peace prize. Whether Nobel committee can do without Gandhi is the question".<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In 1948, the year of Gandhi's death, the Nobel Committee declined to award a prize on the grounds that "there was no suitable living candidate" that year.<sup id="cite_ref-Gandhi_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gandhi-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abrams147-148_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams147-148-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Later, when the <a href="/wiki/14th_Dalai_Lama" title="14th Dalai Lama">14th Dalai Lama</a> was awarded the Peace Prize in 1989, the chairman of the committee said that this was "in part a tribute to the memory of Mahatma Gandhi".<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Other high-profile individuals with widely recognised contributions to peace have been missed out. <i><a href="/wiki/Foreign_Policy" title="Foreign Policy">Foreign Policy</a></i> lists <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A1clav_Havel" title="Václav Havel">Václav Havel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ken_Saro-Wiwa" title="Ken Saro-Wiwa">Ken Saro-Wiwa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sari_Nusseibeh" title="Sari Nusseibeh">Sari Nusseibeh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Corazon_Aquino" title="Corazon Aquino">Corazon Aquino</a> as people who "never won the prize, but should have".<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1965, UN Secretary General <a href="/wiki/U_Thant" title="U Thant">U Thant</a> was informed by the Norwegian Permanent Representative to the UN that he would be awarded that year's prize and asked whether or not he would accept. He consulted staff and later replied that he would. At the same time, Chairman <a href="/wiki/Gunnar_Jahn" title="Gunnar Jahn">Gunnar Jahn</a> of the Nobel Peace prize committee, lobbied heavily against giving U Thant the prize and the prize was at the last minute awarded to <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a>. The rest of the committee all wanted the prize to go to U Thant, for his work in defusing the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a>, ending the war in the Congo, and his ongoing work to mediate an end to the Vietnam War. The disagreement lasted three years and in 1966 and 1967 no prize was given, with Gunnar Jahn effectively vetoing an award to U Thant.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg/170px-Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg/255px-Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg/340px-Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg 2x" data-file-width="570" data-file-height="764" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>James Joyce, one of the controversial omissions of the Literature Prize</div></div></div>
<p>The Literature Prize also has controversial omissions. <a href="/wiki/Adam_Kirsch" title="Adam Kirsch">Adam Kirsch</a> has suggested that many notable writers have missed out on the award for political or extra-literary reasons. The heavy focus on European and Swedish authors has been a subject of criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-KirschNoClue_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KirschNoClue-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The Eurocentric nature of the award was acknowledged by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Englund" title="Peter Englund">Peter Englund</a>, the 2009 Permanent Secretary of the Swedish Academy, as a problem with the award and was attributed to the tendency for the academy to relate more to European authors.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> This tendency towards European authors still leaves some European writers on a list of notable writers that have been overlooked for the Literature Prize, including Europe's <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Chekhov" title="Anton Chekhov">Anton Chekhov</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Zola" title="Émile Zola">Émile Zola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Proust" title="Marcel Proust">Marcel Proust</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Nabokov" title="Vladimir Nabokov">Vladimir Nabokov</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a>, <a href="/wiki/August_Strindberg" title="August Strindberg">August Strindberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simon_Vestdijk" title="Simon Vestdijk">Simon Vestdijk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karel_%C4%8Capek" title="Karel Čapek">Karel Čapek</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">Jorge Luis Borges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Updike" title="John Updike">John Updike</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Miller" title="Arthur Miller">Arthur Miller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a>, and Africa's <a href="/wiki/Chinua_Achebe" title="Chinua Achebe">Chinua Achebe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman56-57_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman56-57-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Candidates can receive multiple nominations the same year. <a href="/wiki/Gaston_Ramon" title="Gaston Ramon">Gaston Ramon</a> received a total of 155<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> nominations in physiology or medicine from 1930 to 1953, the last year with public nomination data for that award as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nobel_Prize&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>. He died in 1963 without being awarded. <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Paul_%C3%89mile_Roux" title="Pierre Paul Émile Roux">Pierre Paul Émile Roux</a> received 115<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> nominations in physiology or medicine, and <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Sommerfeld" title="Arnold Sommerfeld">Arnold Sommerfeld</a> received 84<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> in physics. These are the three most nominated scientists without awards in the data published as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nobel_Prize&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Otto_Stern" title="Otto Stern">Otto Stern</a> received 79<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> nominations in physics 1925–43 before being awarded in 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The strict rule against awarding a prize to more than three people is also controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-Levinovitz61_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levinovitz61-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> When a prize is awarded to recognise an achievement by a team of more than three collaborators, one or more will miss out. For example, in 2002, the prize was awarded to <a href="/wiki/Koichi_Tanaka" title="Koichi Tanaka">Koichi Tanaka</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Fenn_(chemist)" title="John Fenn (chemist)">John Fenn</a> for the development of <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a> in <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein chemistry</a>, an award that did not recognise the achievements of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Hillenkamp" title="Franz Hillenkamp">Franz Hillenkamp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Karas" title="Michael Karas">Michael Karas</a> of the Institute for Physical and Theoretical Chemistry at the <a href="/wiki/Goethe_University_Frankfurt" title="Goethe University Frankfurt">University of Frankfurt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Spinney_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spinney-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> According to one of the nominees for the prize in physics, the three person limit deprived him and two other members of his team of the honor in 2013: the team of <a href="/wiki/C._R._Hagen" title="C. R. Hagen">Carl Hagen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Guralnik" title="Gerald Guralnik">Gerald Guralnik</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kibble" title="Tom Kibble">Tom Kibble</a> published a paper in 1964 that gave answers to how the cosmos began, but did not share the 2013 Physics Prize awarded to <a href="/wiki/Peter_Higgs" title="Peter Higgs">Peter Higgs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Englert" title="François Englert">François Englert</a>, who had also published papers in 1964 concerning the subject. All five physicists arrived at the same conclusion, albeit from different angles. Hagen contends that an equitable solution is to either abandon the three limit restriction, or expand the time period of recognition for a given achievement to two years.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, the prohibition of posthumous awards fails to recognise achievements by an individual or collaborator who dies before the prize is awarded. The Economics Prize was not awarded to <a href="/wiki/Fischer_Black" title="Fischer Black">Fischer Black</a>, who died in 1995, when his co-author <a href="/wiki/Myron_Scholes" title="Myron Scholes">Myron Scholes</a> received the honor in 1997 for their landmark work on option pricing along with <a href="/wiki/Robert_C._Merton" title="Robert C. Merton">Robert C. Merton</a>, another pioneer in the development of valuation of stock options. In the announcement of the award that year, the Nobel committee prominently mentioned Black's key role.
</p><p>Political subterfuge may also deny proper recognition. <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Strassmann" title="Fritz Strassmann">Fritz Strassmann</a>, who co-discovered nuclear fission along with <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a>, may have been denied a share of Hahn's 1944 Nobel Chemistry Award due to having fled Germany when the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazis</a> came to power.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The Meitner and Strassmann roles in the research was not fully recognised until years later, when they joined Hahn in receiving the 1966 <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi_Award" title="Enrico Fermi Award">Enrico Fermi Award</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emphasis_on_discoveries_over_inventions">Emphasis on discoveries over inventions</span></h3>
<p>Alfred Nobel left his fortune to finance annual prizes to be awarded "to those who, during the preceding year, shall have conferred the greatest benefit on mankind".<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> He stated that the Nobel Prizes in Physics should be given "to the person who shall have made the most important 'discovery' or 'invention' within the field of physics". Nobel did not emphasise discoveries, but they have historically been held in higher respect by the Nobel Prize Committee than inventions: 77% of the Physics Prizes have been given to discoveries, compared with only 23% to inventions. Christoph Bartneck and Matthias Rauterberg, in papers published in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> and <i>Technoetic Arts</i>, have argued this emphasis on discoveries has moved the Nobel Prize away from its original intention of rewarding the greatest contribution to society.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_disparity">Gender disparity</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_female_Nobel_laureates" title="List of female Nobel laureates">List of female Nobel laureates</a></div>
<p>In terms of the most prestigious awards in <a href="/wiki/Science,_technology,_engineering,_and_mathematics" title="Science, technology, engineering, and mathematics">STEM</a> fields, only a small proportion have been awarded to women. Out of 210 laureates in Physics, 181 in Chemistry and 216 in Medicine between 1901 and 2018, there were only three female laureates in physics, five in chemistry and 12 in medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Factors proposed to contribute to the discrepancy between this and the roughly equal <a href="/wiki/Human_sex_ratio" title="Human sex ratio">human sex ratio</a> include biased nominations, fewer women than men being active in the relevant fields, Nobel Prizes typically being awarded decades after the research was done (reflecting a time when <a href="/wiki/Gender_bias" class="mw-redirect" title="Gender bias">gender bias</a> in the relevant fields was greater), a greater delay in awarding Nobel Prizes for women's achievements making longevity a more important factor for women (Nobel Prizes are not awarded posthumously), and a tendency to omit women from jointly awarded Nobel Prizes.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Despite these factors, Marie Curie is to date the only person awarded Nobel Prizes in two different sciences (Physics in 1903, Chemistry in 1911); she is one of only three people who have received two Nobel Prizes in sciences (see Multiple laureates below).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specially_distinguished_laureates">Specially distinguished laureates</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiple_laureates">Multiple laureates</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marie_Curie_1903.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A black and white portrait of a woman in profile." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Marie_Curie_1903.jpg/170px-Marie_Curie_1903.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Marie_Curie_1903.jpg/255px-Marie_Curie_1903.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Marie_Curie_1903.jpg 2x" data-file-width="280" data-file-height="396" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marie_Curie_1903.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a>, one of four people who have received the Nobel Prize twice (Physics and Chemistry)</div></div></div>
<p>Four people have received two Nobel Prizes. <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a> received the Physics Prize in 1903 for her work on <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactivity</a> and the Chemistry Prize in 1911 for the isolation of pure <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> making her the only person to be awarded a Nobel Prize in two different sciences. <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a> was awarded the 1954 Chemistry Prize for his research into the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bond</a> and its application to the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_structure" title="Chemical structure">structure</a> of complex substances. Pauling was also awarded the Peace Prize in 1962 for his activism against nuclear weapons, making him the only laureate of two unshared prizes. <a href="/wiki/John_Bardeen" title="John Bardeen">John Bardeen</a> received the Physics Prize twice: in 1956 for the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistor</a> and in 1972 for the theory of <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman180_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman180-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Sanger" title="Frederick Sanger">Frederick Sanger</a> received the prize twice in Chemistry: in 1958 for determining the structure of the <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> molecule and in 1980 for inventing a method of determining base sequences in DNA.<sup id="cite_ref-Shalev78_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shalev78-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Feldman222_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman222-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Two organizations have received the Peace Prize multiple times. The <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a> received it three times: in 1917 and 1944 for its work during the world wars; and in 1963 during the year of its centenary.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams84_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams84-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abrams149_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams149-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abrams199-200_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams199-200-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees" title="United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees">United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees</a> has been awarded the Peace Prize twice for assisting refugees: in 1954 and 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman313_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman313-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_laureates">Family laureates</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Curie_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="Curie Family">Curie family</a> has received the most prizes, with four prizes awarded to five individual laureates. <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a> received the prizes in Physics (in 1903) and Chemistry (in 1911). Her husband, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre Curie</a>, shared the 1903 Physics prize with her.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Their daughter, <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Irène Joliot-Curie</a>, received the Chemistry Prize in 1935 together with her husband <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Joliot-Curie" title="Frédéric Joliot-Curie">Frédéric Joliot-Curie</a>. In addition, the husband of Marie Curie's second daughter, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Richardson_Labouisse,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Richardson Labouisse, Jr.">Henry Labouisse</a>, was the director of <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> when he accepted the Nobel Peace Prize in 1965 on that organisation's behalf.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although no family matches the Curie family's record, there have been several with two laureates. The husband-and-wife team of <a href="/wiki/Gerty_Cori" title="Gerty Cori">Gerty Cori</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Ferdinand_Cori" title="Carl Ferdinand Cori">Carl Ferdinand Cori</a> shared the 1947 Prize in Physiology or Medicine<sup id="cite_ref-GrinsteinBiermann1997_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GrinsteinBiermann1997-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> as did the husband-and-wife team of <a href="/wiki/May-Britt_Moser" title="May-Britt Moser">May-Britt Moser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Moser" title="Edvard Moser">Edvard Moser</a> in 2014 (along with <a href="/wiki/John_O%27Keefe_(neuroscientist)" title="John O&#39;Keefe (neuroscientist)">John O'Keefe</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">J. J. Thomson</a> was awarded the Physics Prize in 1906 for showing that <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> are particles. His son, <a href="/wiki/George_Paget_Thomson" title="George Paget Thomson">George Paget Thomson</a>, received the same prize in 1937 for showing that they <a href="/wiki/Wave%E2%80%93particle_duality" title="Wave–particle duality">also have the properties of waves</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gribbin91_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gribbin91-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Bragg" title="William Henry Bragg">William Henry Bragg</a> and his son, <a href="/wiki/William_Lawrence_Bragg" class="mw-redirect" title="William Lawrence Bragg">William Lawrence Bragg</a>, shared the Physics Prize in 1915 for inventing the <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hargittai2003_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hargittai2003-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a> was awarded the Physics prize in 1922, as was his son, <a href="/wiki/Aage_Bohr" title="Aage Bohr">Aage Bohr</a>, in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manne_Siegbahn" title="Manne Siegbahn">Manne Siegbahn</a>, who received the Physics Prize in 1924, was the father of <a href="/wiki/Kai_Siegbahn" title="Kai Siegbahn">Kai Siegbahn</a>, who received the Physics Prize in 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_169-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hans_von_Euler-Chelpin" title="Hans von Euler-Chelpin">Hans von Euler-Chelpin</a>, who received the Chemistry Prize in 1929, was the father of <a href="/wiki/Ulf_von_Euler" title="Ulf von Euler">Ulf von Euler</a>, who was awarded the Physiology or Medicine Prize in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_169-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/C._V._Raman" title="C. V. Raman">C. V. Raman</a> was awarded the Physics Prize in 1930 and was the uncle of <a href="/wiki/Subrahmanyan_Chandrasekhar" title="Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar">Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar</a>, who was awarded the same prize in 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Feldman406_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman406-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Kornberg" title="Arthur Kornberg">Arthur Kornberg</a> received the Physiology or Medicine Prize in 1959; Kornberg's son, <a href="/wiki/Roger_D._Kornberg" title="Roger D. Kornberg">Roger</a> later received the Chemistry Prize in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jan_Tinbergen" title="Jan Tinbergen">Jan Tinbergen</a>, who was awarded the first Economics Prize in 1969, was the brother of <a href="/wiki/Nikolaas_Tinbergen" title="Nikolaas Tinbergen">Nikolaas Tinbergen</a>, who received the 1973 Physiology or Medicine Prize.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_169-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alva_Myrdal" title="Alva Myrdal">Alva Myrdal</a>, Peace Prize laureate in 1982, was the wife of <a href="/wiki/Gunnar_Myrdal" title="Gunnar Myrdal">Gunnar Myrdal</a> who was awarded the Economics Prize in 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_169-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Economics laureates <a href="/wiki/Paul_Samuelson" title="Paul Samuelson">Paul Samuelson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Arrow" title="Kenneth Arrow">Kenneth Arrow</a> were brothers-in-law. <a href="/wiki/Frits_Zernike" title="Frits Zernike">Frits Zernike</a>, who was awarded the 1953 Physics Prize, is the great-uncle of 1999 Physics laureate <a href="/wiki/Gerard_%27t_Hooft" title="Gerard &#39;t Hooft">Gerard 't Hooft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> In 2019, married couple <a href="/wiki/Abhijit_Banerjee" title="Abhijit Banerjee">Abhijit Banerjee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Esther_Duflo" title="Esther Duflo">Esther Duflo</a> were awarded the Nobel prize for Economics<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Refusals_and_constraints">Refusals and constraints</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A black and white portrait of a man in a suit and tie. Half of his face is in a shadow." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg/170px-Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg/255px-Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg/340px-Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg 2x" data-file-width="401" data-file-height="594" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Richard_Kuhn" title="Richard Kuhn">Richard Kuhn</a>, who was forced to decline his Nobel Prize in Chemistry</div></div></div>
<p>Two laureates have voluntarily declined the Nobel Prize. In 1964, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre" title="Jean-Paul Sartre">Jean-Paul Sartre</a> was awarded the Literature Prize but refused, stating, "A writer must refuse to allow himself to be transformed into an institution, even if it takes place in the most honourable form."<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/L%C3%AA_%C4%90%E1%BB%A9c_Th%E1%BB%8D" title="Lê Đức Thọ">Lê Đức Thọ</a>, chosen for the 1973 Peace Prize for his role in the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Accords" title="Paris Peace Accords">Paris Peace Accords</a>, declined, stating that there was no actual peace in Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a> attempted to decline the prize money while accepting the 1925 Literature Prize; eventually it was agreed to use it to found the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Swedish_Literary_Foundation" title="Anglo-Swedish Literary Foundation">Anglo-Swedish Literary Foundation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Third Reich, Adolf Hitler hindered <a href="/wiki/Richard_Kuhn" title="Richard Kuhn">Richard Kuhn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Butenandt" title="Adolf Butenandt">Adolf Butenandt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Domagk" title="Gerhard Domagk">Gerhard Domagk</a> from accepting their prizes. All of them were awarded their diplomas and gold medals after World War II. In 1958, <a href="/wiki/Boris_Pasternak" title="Boris Pasternak">Boris Pasternak</a> declined his prize for literature due to fear of what the Soviet Union government might do if he travelled to Stockholm to accept his prize. In return, the Swedish Academy refused his refusal, saying "this refusal, of course, in no way alters the validity of the award."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> The Academy announced with regret that the presentation of the Literature Prize could not take place that year, holding it back until 1989 when Pasternak's son accepted the prize on his behalf.<sup id="cite_ref-timesonline-292690_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timesonline-292690-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-washingtonpost_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonpost-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aung_San_Suu_Kyi" title="Aung San Suu Kyi">Aung San Suu Kyi</a> was awarded the Nobel Peace Prize in 1991, but her children accepted the prize because she had been placed under house arrest in <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Burma</a>; Suu Kyi delivered her speech two decades later, in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liu_Xiaobo" title="Liu Xiaobo">Liu Xiaobo</a> was awarded the Nobel Peace Prize in 2010 while he and his wife were under house arrest in China as political prisoners, and he was unable to accept the prize in his lifetime.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">Cultural impact</span></h2>
<p>Being a symbol of scientific or literary achievement that is recognisable worldwide, the Nobel Prize is often depicted in fiction. This includes films like <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prize_(1963_film)" title="The Prize (1963 film)">The Prize</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nobel_Son" title="Nobel Son">Nobel Son</a></i> about fictional Nobel laureates as well as fictionalised accounts of stories surrounding real prizes such as <i><a href="/wiki/Nobel_Chor" title="Nobel Chor">Nobel Chor</a></i>, a film based on the <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore#Theft_of_Nobel_Prize" title="Rabindranath Tagore">theft of Rabindranath Tagore's prize</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nobel_film_screen_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobel_film_screen-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The memorial symbol "Planet of Alfred Nobel" was opened in <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dnipropetrovsk_University&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dnipropetrovsk University (page does not exist)">Dnipropetrovsk University</a> of Economics and Law in 2008. On the globe, there are 802 Nobel laureates' reliefs made of a composite alloy obtained when disposing of military strategic missiles.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
